	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Black British</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the purpose of harmonising results to make them comparable across the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">ONS</a> includes individuals in Scotland who classified themselves in the "African" category (29,638 people), which in the Scottish version of the census is separate from "Caribbean or Black" (6,540 people),<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> in this "Black or Black British" category. The ONS note that "the African categories used in Scotland could potentially capture White/Asian/Other African in addition to Black identities".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Black British people</b> are <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> citizens of either Black <a href="/wiki/Black_African" class="mw-redirect" title="Black African">African</a> background, or of Black <a href="/wiki/British_African-Caribbean_people" title="British African-Caribbean people">African-Caribbean</a> (sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Afro-Caribbean" title="Afro-Caribbean">Afro-Caribbean</a>") background and include people with mixed ancestry from either group.<sup id="cite_ref-Gadsby_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gadsby-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The term developed in the 1950s, referring to the Black British West Indian people from the former Caribbean <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British colonies</a> in the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">West Indies</a> (i.e.,&#160;the <a href="/wiki/New_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="New Commonwealth">New Commonwealth</a>) now referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Windrush_Generation" class="mw-redirect" title="Windrush Generation">Windrush Generation</a>, and people from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, who are residents of the United Kingdom and are British. 
</p><p>The term <b>black</b> has historically had a number of applications as a racial and political label and may be used in a wider sociopolitical context to encompass a broader range of non-European <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_minority" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic minority">ethnic minority</a> populations in Britain. This has become a controversial definition.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> "Black British" is one of various self-designation entries used in official <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_ethnicity_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Classification of ethnicity in the United Kingdom">UK ethnicity classifications</a>.
</p><p>Black residents constituted around 3 per cent of the United Kingdom's population in 2011. The figures have increased from the 1991 census when 1.63% of the population were recorded as Black or Black British to 1.15 million residents in 2001, or 2 per cent of the population, this further increased to just over 1.9 million in 2011. Over 95% of Black British live in England, particularly in England's larger urban areas, with most (over a million) Black British living in <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Greater London</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Historically, the term has most commonly been used to refer to Black people of <a href="/wiki/New_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="New Commonwealth">New Commonwealth</a> origin, of both West African and South Asian descent. For example, <a href="/wiki/Southall_Black_Sisters" title="Southall Black Sisters">Southall Black Sisters</a> was established in 1979 "to meet the needs of black (Asian and Afro-Caribbean) women".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Note that <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">"Asian" in the British context</a> usually refers to people of <a href="/wiki/South_Asian" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asian">South Asian</a> ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BSA_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BSA-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
"Black" was used in this inclusive political sense to mean "non-<a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">white British</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-verma_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verma-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, a time of rising activism against racial discrimination, the main communities so described were from the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Solidarity against <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> and discrimination sometimes extended the term at that time to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_community_in_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish community in Britain">Irish population of Britain</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several organisations continue to use the term inclusively, such as the Black Arts Alliance,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> who extend their use of the term to Latin Americans and all refugees,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Black_Police_Association_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Black Police Association (UK)">National Black Police Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The official <a href="/wiki/Census_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Census in the United Kingdom">UK Census</a> has separate self-designation entries for respondents to identify as "Asian British", "Black British" and "Other ethnic group".<sup id="cite_ref-Onseg_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onseg-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Non-resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin" title="Non-resident Indian and person of Indian origin">Indian diaspora</a> and in particular <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_Asians_from_Uganda" title="Expulsion of Asians from Uganda">expulsion of Asians from Uganda</a> in 1972, many <a href="/wiki/British_Asians" class="mw-redirect" title="British Asians">British Asians</a> are from families that had previously lived for several generations in the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indian_diaspora_in_Southeast_Africa" title="Indian diaspora in Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Census_classification">Census classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Census classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_1991" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 1991">1991 UK census</a> was the first to include a <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_ethnicity_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Classification of ethnicity in the United Kingdom">question on ethnicity</a>. As of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 UK Census</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> (ONS) and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Statistics_and_Research_Agency" title="Northern Ireland Statistics and Research Agency">Northern Ireland Statistics and Research Agency</a> (NISRA) allow people in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> who self-identify as "Black" to select "Black African", "Black Caribbean" or "Any other Black/African/Caribbean background" tick boxes.<sup id="cite_ref-Onseg_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onseg-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> For the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011#2011_Census_for_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Scottish census</a>, the <a href="/wiki/General_Register_Office_for_Scotland" title="General Register Office for Scotland">General Register Office for Scotland</a> (GOS) also established new, separate "African, African Scottish or African British" and "Caribbean, Caribbean Scottish or Caribbean British" tick boxes for individuals in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> from Africa and the Caribbean, respectively, who do not identify as "Black, Black Scottish or Black British".<sup id="cite_ref-Snoec_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snoec-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In all of the UK censuses, persons with multiple familial ancestries can write in their respective ethnicities under a "Mixed or multiple ethnic groups" option, which includes additional "White and Black Caribbean" or "White and Black African" tick boxes in England, Wales, and Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-Onseg_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onseg-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_usage">Historical usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Historical usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Black British was also a term for those Black and <a href="/wiki/Mixed-race" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> people in <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone_Krio_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Leone Krio people">Krio</a>) who were descendants of migrants from England and Canada and identified as British.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> They are generally the descendants of black people who lived in England in the 18th century and freed Black American slaves who fought for the Crown in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Black_Loyalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Loyalists">Black Loyalists</a>). In 1787, hundreds of London's <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Relief_of_the_Black_Poor" title="Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor">black poor</a> (a category that included the <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">East Indian</a> seamen known as <a href="/wiki/Lascars" class="mw-redirect" title="Lascars">lascars</a>) agreed to go to this West African colony on the condition that they would retain the status of <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British subjects</a>, live in freedom under the protection of the <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">British Crown</a>, and be defended by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>. Making this fresh start with them were some white people (see also <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Relief_of_the_Black_Poor" title="Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor">Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor</a>), including lovers, wives, and widows of the black men.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In addition, nearly 1200 Black Loyalists, former American slaves who had been freed and resettled in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, also chose to join the new colony.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_African-Caribbean_people" title="British African-Caribbean people">British African-Caribbean people</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is evidence of people with African (including <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a>) ancestry in Roman Britain. A craniometric study of 22 individuals from <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>, Roman London, found that four of them appeared to be of African ancestry, and the isotopic analysis of their bones suggested childhoods spent in a climate warmer than Roman Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Analysis of autosomal DNA from four individuals from Roman London found that one had Black ancestry, with brown eyes and dark brown or black hair. Bone isotopes suggested that this individual, a male aged over 45 years, had spent his childhood in the London region.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Bangle_Lady" title="Ivory Bangle Lady">Ivory Bangle Lady</a> whose rich burial was found in York also had cranial features that hinted at a 'mixed' white/black ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Her <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> was made of stone and also contained a jet bracelet and an ivory bangle, indicating great wealth for the time.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roman_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roman-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
There is written evidence of the presence in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> of residents from multiethnic <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Romanised North Africa</a>. The inscriptions suggest that most of these inhabitants were involved with the military. Some were in the upper echelons of society.
</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Augustan History</a></i>, Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimus Severus</a> supposedly visited <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> in 210 AD.  While returning from an inspection of the wall, he was said to have been mocked by an "Ethiope" soldier holding a garland of cypress-boughs.  Severus ordered him away, reportedly being "frightened"<sup id="cite_ref-:7_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> by his dark skin colour<sup id="cite_ref-:7_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and seeing his act and appearance as an omen. The "Ethiope" is written to have said: "You have been all things, you have conquered all things, now, O conqueror, be a god."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> However, it is uncertain if the incident actually took place as it was written about in the late roman period, centuries later.
</p><p>In 1953, the remains of the <a href="/wiki/Beachy_Head_Lady" title="Beachy Head Lady">Beachy Head Lady</a> were found in East Sussex. The skeleton, which is thought to have originated from <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, has been dated to around 245 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-culture_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-culture-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Saxon_England">Anglo-Saxon England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Anglo-Saxon England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A skeleton of a female found in England may indicate the existence of a very small number of black people in Britain dating to the 11th century.  In 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-:5_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> a skeleton was discovered in <a href="/wiki/Fairford" title="Fairford">Fairford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Forensic_anthropology" title="Forensic anthropology">forensic anthropology</a> revealed to be that of a sub-Saharan African woman. Her remains have been dated between the years 896 and 1025.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Local historians believe she may have been a bonded servant.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="16th_century">16th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 16th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early in the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a> likely brought servants from Africa among her retinue when she travelled to England to marry <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a>. A black musician is among the six trumpeters depicted in the royal retinue of Henry VIII in the Westminster Tournament Roll, an illuminated manuscript dating from 1511. He wears the royal <a href="/wiki/Livery" title="Livery">livery</a> and is mounted on horseback. The man is generally identified as the "<a href="/wiki/John_Blanke" title="John Blanke">John Blanke</a>, the blacke trumpeter," who is listed in the payment accounts of both Henry&#160;VIII and his father, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry&#160;VII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HttpwwwblackpresencecoukjohnblankeJohn_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HttpwwwblackpresencecoukjohnblankeJohn-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> A group of Africans were at the court of <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV of Scotland</a>, including a drummer referred to as the "More Taubronar". Both he and John Blanke were paid wages for their services.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
When trade lines began to open between London and West Africa, persons from this area began coming to Britain on board merchant and slaving ships. For example, merchant <a href="/wiki/John_Lok" title="John Lok">John Lok</a> brought several captives to London in 1555 from Guinea. The voyage account in <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hakluyt" title="Richard Hakluyt">Hakluyt</a> reports that they: "were tall and strong men, and could wel agree with our meates and drinkes. The colde and moyst aire doth somewhat offend them."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg/220px-Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg/330px-Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg/440px-Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="444" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Trumpeter_at_Henry_VIII%27s_Tournament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extract from <a href="/wiki/The_1511_Westminster_Tournament_Roll" title="The 1511 Westminster Tournament Roll">the Westminster Tournament Roll</a> almost certainly showing <a href="/wiki/John_Blanke" title="John Blanke">John Blanke</a>, the only figure wearing a brown turban latticed with yellow.</div></div></div><p>During the later 16th century as well as into the first two decades of the 17th century, 25 people named in the records of the small parish of St. Botolph's in <a href="/wiki/Aldgate" title="Aldgate">Aldgate</a> are identified as "blackamoors."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the period of the war with Spain, between 1588 and 1604, there was an increase in the number of people reaching England from Spanish colonial expeditions in parts of Africa. The English freed many of these captives from enslavement on Spanish ships. They arrived in England largely as a by-product of the slave trade; some were of mixed-race African and Spanish, and became interpreters or sailors.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> American historian <a href="/wiki/Ira_Berlin" title="Ira Berlin">Ira Berlin</a> classified such persons as <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Creole" title="Atlantic Creole">Atlantic Creoles</a> or the Charter Generation of slaves and multi-racial workers in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Slaver <a href="/wiki/John_Hawkins_(naval_commander)" title="John Hawkins (naval commander)">John Hawkins</a> arrived in London with 300 captives from West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> However, the slave trade did not become entrenched until the 17th century and Hawkins only embarked on three expeditions.
</p><p>Blackamoor servants were perceived as a fashionable novelty and were popular in the homes of the wealthy, including that of Queen Elizabeth I.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Among these servants was "John Come-quick, a blackemore", servant to Capt Thomas Love.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Others included in parish registers include Domingo "a black neigro servaunt unto Sir William Winter", buried the xxviith daye of August [1587] and "Frauncis a Blackamoor servant to Thomas Parker", buried in January 1591.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Some were free workers, although most were employed as servants and entertainers to the wealthy. Some worked in ports, but were invariably described as chattel labour.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The black population may have been several hundred during the Elizabethan period, though their settlement was actively discouraged by Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>'s privy council.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Archival evidence shows records of more than 360 African people between 1500 to 1640 in England and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Reacting to the darker complexion of people with biracial parentage, George Best argued in 1578 that black skin was not related to the heat of the sun (in Africa) but was instead caused by biblical damnation. Reginald Scot later associated black skin with witchcraft, describing (in his book <i>Discoverie of Witchcraft</i>) an unprepossessing devil in 1584 as having "horns on his head, fire in his mouth, a tail, eyes like a bison, fangs like a dog, claws like a bear, a skin like a niger and a voice roaring like a lion". These views existed across British society, including among playwrights and royalty, exemplified by the fact black slaves were used as some of the coach porters of King James VI of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In part because they were generally not Christian, black people were seen as fundamentally different, and therefore common virtues did not so readily apply.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In addition, in this period, England had no concept of naturalization as a means of incorporating immigrants into the society. It conceived of English subjects as those people born on the island. Those who were not would never be considered subjects or citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-Banks_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banks-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1596, Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>'s privy council issued letters to the lord mayors of major cities asserting that "of late divers blackmoores brought into this realm, of which kind of people there are already here to manie...". Sir Thomas Sherley and Caspar Van Senden, a merchant of Lübeck, attempted to capitalise on this by petitioning Elizabeth I's Privy Council to allow them to transport slaves they had captured in Africa to <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, presumably to sell them there. The relevant Privy Council Letters of July 1596 and a draft proclamation from the papers of Robert Cecil have been presented as an attempt to deport these captives from England.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  However, Van Senden and Sherley did not succeed in this effort, as they acknowledged in correspondence with Sir Robert Cecil.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  In 1601, Elizabeth issued another proclamation expressing that she was "highly discontented to understand the great number of Negroes and blackamoors which (as she is informed) are carried into this realm",<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and again licensing van Senden to deport them. Her proclamation of 1601 stated that the blackamoors were "fostered and powered here, to the great annoyance of [the queen's] own liege people, that covet the relief, which those people consume". It further stated that "most of them are infidels, having no understanding of Christ or his Gospel".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In March 2019 two of the skeletons found on the <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Rose" title="Mary Rose">Mary Rose</a></i> were found to have Southern European or North African ancestry; one found to be wearing a leather wrist-guard bearing the arms of Catherine of Aragon and royal arms of England is thought to possibly be Spanish or North African, the other, known as "Henry" was thought to also have similar genetic makeup. Henry’s mitochondrial DNA showed that his ancestry may have came from Southern Europe, the Near East, or North Africa.  Although, Dr Sam Robson from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Portsmouth" title="University of Portsmouth">University of Portsmouth</a> "ruled out" that Henry was black or that he was <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan African</a> in origin. Dr Onyeka Nubia cautioned that the number of those on board the <i>Mary Rose</i> that had heritage beyond Britain was not necessarily representative of the whole of England at the time, although it definitely was not a "one-off".<sup id="cite_ref-:8_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> It is thought they are likely to have travelled through Spain or Portugal before arriving in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacques_Francis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jacques Francis (page does not exist)">Jacques Francis</a>, who has been described as a slave by some historians,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> but described himself in Latin as a "famulus", meaning servant, slave or  attendant.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Francis was born on an island off the coast of Guinea, likely <a href="/wiki/Arguin" title="Arguin">Arguin Island</a>, off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He worked as a diver for Pietro Paulo Corsi in his salvage operations on the sunken <i>St Mary</i> and <i>St Edward</i> of <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> and other ships, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Rose" title="Mary Rose">Mary Rose</a></i>, which had sunk in Portsmouth harbour. When Corsi was accused of theft, Francis stood by him in an English court. With help from an interpreter, he supported his master's claims of innocence. Some of the depositions in the case displayed negative attitudes towards slaves or black people as witnesses.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies of blackamoors in <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Britain" title="Early modern Britain">early modern Britain</a> indicate a minor continuing presence. Such studies include Imtiaz Habib's <i>Black Lives in the English Archives, 1500–1677: Imprints of the Invisible</i><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> (Ashgate, 2008), <a href="/wiki/Onyeka" title="Onyeka">Onyeka</a>'s <i>Blackamoores: Africans in Tudor England, Their Presence, Status and Origins</i><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> (Narrative Eye, 2013), and Miranda Kaufmann's Oxford DPhil thesis <i>Africans in Britain, 1500–1640 ,</i><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Black Tudors: The Untold Story</i> (Oneworld, 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_and_18th_centuries">17th and 18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 17th and 18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg/220px-Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg/330px-Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg/440px-Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_Murray_Countess_Dysart_painting.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1651 painting of Scottish noblewoman <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Maitland,_Duchess_of_Lauderdale" title="Elizabeth Maitland, Duchess of Lauderdale">Elizabeth Maitland, Duchess of Lauderdale</a> with her black servant.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_slave_trade">The slave trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The slave trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1687, a "Moor" was given the freedom of the city of <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>. He is listed in the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">freemen's rolls</a> as "John Moore - blacke".  He is the only black man or woman to have been found to date in the <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a> rolls.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FourTimesNoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/FourTimesNoon.jpg/220px-FourTimesNoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/FourTimesNoon.jpg/330px-FourTimesNoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/FourTimesNoon.jpg/440px-FourTimesNoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="516" data-file-height="606" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FourTimesNoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a>'s engraving <i><a href="/wiki/Four_Times_of_the_Day" title="Four Times of the Day">Four Times of the Day</a>: Noon</i> (1738) shows a black London resident (on the left).</div></div></div>
<p>At this time there was an increase in black settlement in London. Britain was involved with the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">tri-continental slave trade</a> between Europe, Africa and the Americas. Black slaves were attendants to sea captains and ex-colonial officials, as well as traders, plantation owners and military personnel. This caused an increasing black presence in the northern, eastern, and southern areas of London. One of the most famous slaves to attend a sea captain was known as <a href="/wiki/Sambo%27s_Grave" title="Sambo&#39;s Grave">Sambo</a>. He fell ill shortly after arriving in England and was consequently buried in Lancashire. His plaque and gravestone still stand to this day. There were also small numbers of free slaves and seamen from West Africa and South Asia. Many of these people were forced into beggary due to the lack of jobs and racial discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The involvement of merchants from <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a><sup id="cite_ref-Black_Liverpool_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black_Liverpool-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Transatlantic slave trade">transatlantic slave trade</a> was the most important factor in the development of the Black British community. These communities flourished in port cities strongly involved in the slave trade, such as <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a><sup id="cite_ref-Black_Liverpool_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black_Liverpool-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>. By 1795, Liverpool had 62.5 per cent of the European Slave Trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Black_Liverpool_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black_Liverpool-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Liverpool is home to Britain's oldest black community, dating at least to the 1730s. Some Liverpudlians are able to trace their black heritage in the city back ten generations.<sup id="cite_ref-Black_Liverpool_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black_Liverpool-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Early black settlers in the city included seamen, the mixed-race children of traders sent to be educated in England, servants, and freed slaves. Mistaken references to slaves entering the country after 1722 being deemed to be free men are derived from a source in which 1722 is a misprint for 1772, in turn based on a misunderstanding of the results of the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_v_Stewart" title="Somerset v Stewart">Somerset case</a> referred to below.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre-Brown_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-Brown-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1787, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Clarkson" title="Thomas Clarkson">Thomas Clarkson</a>, an English abolitionist, noted at a speech in Manchester: "I was surprised also to find a great crowd of black people standing round the pulpit. There might be forty or fifty of them."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence that black men and women were occasionally discriminated against when dealing with the law because of their skin colour. In 1737, George Scipio was accused of stealing Anne Godfrey's washing, the case rested entirely on whether or not Scipio was the only black man in Hackney at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a>, black writer, composer, shopkeeper and voter in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a> wrote, that despite being in Britain since the age of two he felt he was "only a lodger, and hardly that."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Sancho complained of "the national antipathy and prejudice" of native white Britons "towards their wooly headed brethren."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Sancho was frustrated that many resorted to stereotyping their black neighbours.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A financially-independent householder, he became the first black person of African origin to vote in parliamentary elections in Britain, in a time when only 3% of the British population were allowed to vote.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnspencer1708.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Johnspencer1708.jpg/220px-Johnspencer1708.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Johnspencer1708.jpg/330px-Johnspencer1708.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Johnspencer1708.jpg 2x" data-file-width="370" data-file-height="549" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnspencer1708.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1744 painting of Hon. <a href="/wiki/John_Spencer_(British_politician)" title="John Spencer (British politician)">John Spencer</a>, his only son and their black servant.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1764, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gentleman%27s_Magazine" title="The Gentleman&#39;s Magazine">The Gentleman's Magazine</a></i> reported that there was "supposed to be near 20,000 Negroe servants." It was reported in the Morning Gazette that there was 30,000 in the country as a whole, though the numbers were thought to be "alarmist" exaggerations. In the same year, a party for black men and women in a <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Street" title="Fleet Street">Fleet Street</a> pub was sufficiently unusual to be written about in the newspapers. Their presence in the country was striking enough to start heated outbreaks of distaste for <i>colonies of <a href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" title="Khoikhoi">Hottentots</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Modern historians estimate, based on parish lists, baptismal and marriage registers as well as criminal and sales contracts, that about 10,000 black people lived in Britain during the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Other estimates put the number at 15,000.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 1772, <a href="/wiki/William_Murray,_1st_Earl_of_Mansfield" title="William Murray, 1st Earl of Mansfield">Lord Mansfield</a> put the number of black people in the country at as many as 15,000, though most modern historians consider 10,000 to be the most likely.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The black population was estimated at around 10,000 in London, making black people approximately 1% of the overall London population. The black population constituted around 0.1% of the total population of Britain in 1780.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_First_Black_Britons_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_First_Black_Britons-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The black female population is estimated to have barely reached 20% of the overall Afro-Caribbean population in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-The_First_Black_Britons_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_First_Black_Britons-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In the 1780s with the end of the American Revolutionary War, hundreds of black loyalists from America were resettled in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Later some emigrated to Sierre Leonne with help from <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Relief_of_the_Black_Poor" title="Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor">Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor</a> after suffering destitution.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ignatius_Sancho,_1768.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg/220px-Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg/330px-Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg/440px-Ignatius_Sancho%2C_1768.jpg 2x" data-file-width="641" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ignatius_Sancho,_1768.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composer and shopkeeper <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a>  was the first black person of African origins to vote in parliamentary elections and became a symbol of the humanity of Africans and immorality of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">slave trade</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Officially, slavery was never legal in England.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Slavery_at_common_law#Cartwright&#39;s_case" title="Slavery at common law">Cartwright decision</a> of 1569 resolved that England was "too pure an air for a slave to breathe in". However, black African slaves continued to be bought and sold in England during the eighteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The slavery issue was not legally contested until the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_v_Stewart" title="Somerset v Stewart">Somerset case</a> of 1772, which concerned James Somersett, a fugitive black slave from <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. Lord Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/William_Murray,_1st_Earl_of_Mansfield" title="William Murray, 1st Earl of Mansfield">William Murray, 1st Earl of Mansfield</a> concluded that Somerset could not be forced to leave England against his will. He later reiterated: "The determinations go no further than that the master cannot by force compel him to go out of the kingdom."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> (See generally, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_at_common_law" title="Slavery at common law">Slavery at common law</a>.) Despite the previous rulings, such as the 1706 declaration (which was clarified a year later) by <a href="/wiki/John_Holt_(Lord_Chief_Justice)" title="John Holt (Lord Chief Justice)">Lord Chief Justice Holt</a><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> on slavery not being legal in Britain, it was often ignored, with the argument that the slaves were property and therefore could not be considered people.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Slave owner Thomas Papillon was one of many who took his black servant "to be in the nature and quality of my goods and chattel".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In 1731 the Lord Mayor of London ruled that "no Negroes shall be bound apprentices to any Tradesman or Artificer of this City". Due to this ruling, most were forced into working as servants.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandhu_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandhu-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Those black Londoners who were unpaid servants were in effect slaves in anything but name.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the 1750s, London became the home of many Blacks, as well as Jews, Irish, Germans and <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Gretchen_Gerzina" title="Gretchen Gerzina">Gretchen Gerzina</a> in her <i>Black London</i>, by the mid-18th century, Blacks accounted for somewhere between one and three per cent of the London populace.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartels_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartels-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of the number of Black residents in the city has been found through registered burials. Some black people in London resisted slavery through escape.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartels_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartels-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Leading Black activists of this era included <a href="/wiki/Olaudah_Equiano" title="Olaudah Equiano">Olaudah Equiano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quobna_Ottobah_Cugoano" class="mw-redirect" title="Quobna Ottobah Cugoano">Quobna Ottobah Cugoano</a>.
</p><p>With the support of other Britons, these activists demanded that Blacks be freed from slavery. Supporters involved in these movements included workers and other nationalities of the urban poor. London Blacks vocally contested slavery and the slave trade. At this time, the slavery of whites was forbidden, but the legal statuses of these practices were not clearly defined.
</p><p>During this era, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mansfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mansfield">Lord Mansfield</a> declared that a slave who fled from his master could not be taken by force in England, nor sold abroad. Mansfield was at pains to point out that his ruling did not abolish slavery itself in eighteenth century England.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This verdict fuelled the numbers of Blacks who escaped slavery, and helped send slavery into decline. During this same period, many former American slave soldiers, who had fought on the side of the British in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_American_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="War of American Independence">American Revolutionary War</a>, were resettled as free men in London. They were never awarded pensions, and many of them became poverty-stricken and were reduced to begging on the streets. Reports at the time stated that they "had no prospect of subsisting in this country but by depredations on the public, or by common charity". A sympathetic observer wrote that "great numbers of Blacks and People of Colour, many of them refugees from America and others who have by land or sea been in his Majesty's service were...in great distress." Even towards white loyalists there was little good will to new arrivals from America.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The blacks in London lived among whites in areas of <a href="/wiki/Mile_End" title="Mile End">Mile End</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stepney" title="Stepney">Stepney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paddington" title="Paddington">Paddington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St_Giles,_London" title="St Giles, London">St Giles</a>. After Mansfield's ruling many former slaves continued to work for their old masters as paid employees. Between 14,000 and 15,000 (then contemporary estimates) slaves were immediately freed in England.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Many of these emancipated individuals became labelled as the "black poor", the black poor were defined as former slave soldiers since emancipated, seafarers, such as South Asian lascars,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> former indentured servants and former indentured plantation workers.<sup id="cite_ref-FilePower_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FilePower-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Harris%27s_List_of_Covent_Garden_Ladies" title="Harris&#39;s List of Covent Garden Ladies">In <i>Harris's List of Covent Garden Ladies</i>,</a>  published from 1757 to 1795, an annual directory of <a href="/wiki/Prostitutes" class="mw-redirect" title="Prostitutes">prostitutes</a> then working in <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian</a> <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, a '<a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">'mulatto</a>'' woman is described in 1789 as follows: ''Eliza is of a dark complexion, being a downright mulatto, and intimates by the thickness of her hair and her eyebrows, that she is well-furred below. ....Can produce an excellent tale, which she charges little more for than it is well worth,considering the scarcity of its complexion in this country, being but very lately imported from the West Indies.''<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the late 18th century, numerous publications and memoirs were written about the "black poor". One example is the writings of Equiano, a former slave who became an unofficial spokesman for Britain's Black community. His memoir about his life entitled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Interesting_Narrative_of_the_Life_of_Olaudah_Equiano" title="The Interesting Narrative of the Life of Olaudah Equiano">The Interesting Narrative of the Life of Olaudah Equiano</a></i>.
</p><p>In 1786, Olaudah Equiano became the first black person to be employed by the British government, when he was made Commissary of Provisions and Stores for the 350 black people suffering from poverty who had decided to accept the government's offer of an assisted passage to Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The following year, in 1787, encouraged by the <a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Relief_of_the_Black_Poor" title="Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor">Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor</a>, 4,000<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> black Londoners were aided in emigrating to <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> in West Africa, founding the first British colony on the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> They asked that their status as <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British subjects</a> be recognized, along with the duty of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> to defend them. It is possible a desire to remove black people from London<sup><a href="/wiki/Committee_for_the_Relief_of_the_Black_Poor#cite_note-2" title="Committee for the Relief of the Black Poor">[2]</a></sup> was a principal goal of the committee. There was a prevalent view among the contemporary British West Indian <a href="/wiki/Plantocracy" title="Plantocracy">plantocracy</a> that racial intermarriage was abhorrent. The chair of the committee wrote to the Standing Committee of West India Planters and Merchants requesting their advice and assistance in procuring an act of parliament to "prevent any Foreign Blacks being brought to this country to remain".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An Indian Briton, <a href="/wiki/Dadabhai_Naoroji" title="Dadabhai Naoroji">Dadabhai Naoroji</a>, stood for election to <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">parliament</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> in 1886. He was defeated, leading the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">leader of the Conservative Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> to remark that "however great the progress of mankind has been, and however far we have advanced in overcoming prejudices, I doubt if we have yet got to the point of view where a British constituency would elect a Blackman".<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> This led to much discussion about the applicability of the term "black" to South Asians. Naoroji was elected to parliament in 1892, becoming the first Member of Parliament (MP) of Indian descent.
</p><p>
In 2007, scientists found the rare paternal <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_A_(Y-DNA)#A1a-M31" title="Haplogroup A (Y-DNA)">haplogroup A1</a> in a few living British men with Yorkshire surnames. This clade is today almost exclusively found among males in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, where it is also rare. The haplogroup is thought to have been brought to Britain either through enlisted soldiers during Roman Britain, or much later via the modern <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>. Turi King, a co-author on the study, noted the most probable ''guess'' was the West African slave trade. Some of the known individuals who arrived through the slave route, such as <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olaudah_Equiano" title="Olaudah Equiano">Olaudah Equiano</a>, attained a very high social rank. Some married into the general population.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pablo_Fanque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Pablo_Fanque.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="263" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="263" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pablo_Fanque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pablo_Fanque" title="Pablo Fanque">Pablo Fanque</a>, celebrated circus owner and performer in Victorian Britain.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late 18th century, the British slave trade declined in response to changing popular opinion. Both Great Britain and the United States abolished the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> in 1808, and cooperated in liberating slaves from illegal trading ships off the coast of West Africa. Many of these freed slaves were taken to Sierra Leone for settlement. Slavery was abolished completely in the British Empire by 1834, although it had been profitable on Caribbean plantations. Fewer blacks were brought into London from the West Indies and West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FilePower_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FilePower-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The resident British black population, primarily male, was no longer growing from the trickle of slaves and servants from the West Indies and America.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Abolition meant a virtual halt to the arrival of black people to Britain, just as immigration from Europe was increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>  The black population of Victorian Britain was so small that those living outside of larger trading ports were isolated from the black population.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The mentioning of black people and descendants in parish registers declined markedly in the early 19th century.  It is possible that researchers simply did not collect the data or that the mostly black male population of the late 18th century had married white women.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Evidence of such marriages may still be found today with descendants of black servants such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Barber" title="Francis Barber">Francis Barber</a>, a Jamaican-born servant who lived in Britain during the 18th century. His descendants still live in England today and are white.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandhu_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandhu-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Abolition of slavery in 1833, effectively ended the period of small-scale black immigration to London and Britain. Though, there were some exceptions, black and Chinese seamen began putting down the roots of small communities in British ports, not least because they were abandoned there by their employers.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 19th century, race discrimination was furthered by theories of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a>, which held that whites were the superior race and that blacks were less intelligent than whites. Attempts to support these theories cited 'scientific evidence', such as brain size. James Hunt, President of the London Anthropological Society, in 1863 in his paper "On the Negro's place in nature" wrote,"the Negro is inferior intellectually to the European...[and] can only be humanised and civilised by Europeans.''<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In the 1880s, there was a build-up of small groups of black dockside communities in towns such as <a href="/wiki/Canning_Town" title="Canning Town">Canning Town</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Liverpool and <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_(15_September_1862)_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_%2815_September_1862%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="541" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sara_Forbes_Bonetta_(15_September_1862)_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sara_Forbes_Bonetta" title="Sara Forbes Bonetta">Sara Forbes Bonetta</a>, West African princess and goddaughter to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>. Orphaned in intertribal warfare, given as a "gift" to Queen Victoria.</div></div></div><p> Despite social prejudice and discrimination in Victorian England, some 19th-century black Britons achieved exceptional success. <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Fanque" title="Pablo Fanque">Pablo Fanque</a>, born poor as William Darby in <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a>, rose to become the proprietor of one of Britain's most successful Victorian circuses. He is immortalised in the lyrics of <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Being_for_the_Benefit_of_Mr._Kite!" title="Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!">Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!</a>" Thirty years after his 1871 death, the chaplain of the Showman's Guild said: 
</p><blockquote><p>"In the great brotherhood of the equestrian world there is no colour line [bar], for, although Pablo Fanque was of African extraction, he speedily made his way to the top of his profession. The camaraderie of the ring has but one test – ability."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Another great <a href="/wiki/Circus" title="Circus">circus</a> performer was equestrian Joseph Hillier, who took over and ran <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ducrow" title="Andrew Ducrow">Andrew Ducrow</a>'s circus company after Ducrow died.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Tull23.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walter_Tull23.jpg/220px-Walter_Tull23.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walter_Tull23.jpg/330px-Walter_Tull23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walter_Tull23.jpg/440px-Walter_Tull23.jpg 2x" data-file-width="615" data-file-height="434" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Tull23.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Walter_Tull" title="Walter Tull">Walter Tull</a>, professional footballer and mixed-heritage British Army officer, pictured with fellow officers.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg/220px-British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg/330px-British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg/440px-British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5297" data-file-height="3855" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_West_Indies_Regiment_Q_001202.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies_Regiment" title="British West Indies Regiment">British West Indies Regiment</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Somme</a>, September 1916. All of the men pictured were Afro-Caribbean people who volunteered to fight for the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> saw a small growth in the size of London's Black communities with the arrival of merchant seamen and soldiers. At that time, there were also small groups of students from Africa and the Caribbean migrating into London. These communities are now among the oldest black communities of London.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The largest Black communities were to be found in the United Kingdom's great port cities: London's <a href="/wiki/East_End" class="mw-redirect" title="East End">East End</a>, Liverpool, Bristol and Cardiff's <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Bay" title="Tiger Bay">Tiger Bay</a>, with other communities in <a href="/wiki/South_Shields" title="South Shields">South Shields</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tyne_%26_Wear" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyne &amp; Wear">Tyne &amp; Wear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>. In 1914, the black population was estimated at 10,000 and centered largely in London, with around 70% being men.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> These residents had for the most part emigrated from parts of the British Empire. The number of black soldiers serving in the British army prior to World War I is unknown but was likely to have been negligibly low.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> One of the Black British soldiers during World War I was <a href="/wiki/Walter_Tull" title="Walter Tull">Walter Tull</a>, an English professional footballer, born to a <a href="/wiki/Barbadian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbadian people">Barbadian</a> carpenter Daniel Tull and Kent-born Alice Elizabeth Palmer. His grandfather was a slave in <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Tull became the first British-born mixed-heritage infantry officer in a regular British Army regiment, despite the 1914 Manual of Military Law specifically excluding soldiers that were not "of pure European descent" from becoming commissioned officers.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Colonial soldiers and sailors of Afro-Caribbean descent served here during the war and some settled in these cities. The South Shields community—which also included other "coloured" seamen known as lascars, who were from South Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>—were victims of the UK's first <a href="/wiki/Race_riot" class="mw-redirect" title="Race riot">race riot</a> in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Soon eight other cities with significant non-white communities were also hit by race riots.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabili_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabili-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Due to these disturbances, many of the residents from the Arab world as well as some other immigrants were evacuated to their homelands.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In that first postwar summer, other racial riots of whites against "coloured" peoples also took place in numerous United States cities, towns in the Caribbean, and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Tabili_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabili-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> They were part of the social dislocation after the war as societies struggled to integrate veterans into the work forces again, and groups competed for jobs and housing. At Australian insistence, the British refused to accept the <a href="/wiki/Racial_Equality_Proposal" title="Racial Equality Proposal">Racial Equality Proposal</a> put forward by the Japanese at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference, 1919</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>World War II marked another period of growth for the black communities in London, Liverpool and elsewhere in Britain. Many blacks from the Caribbean and West Africa arrived in small groups as wartime workers, merchant seamen, and servicemen from the army, navy, and air forces.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> For example, in February 1941, 345 West Indians came to work in factories in and around Liverpool, making munitions.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> By the end of 1943 there were 3,312 African-American <a href="/wiki/G.I._(military)" title="G.I. (military)">GIs</a> based at <a href="/wiki/Maghull" title="Maghull">Maghull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huyton" title="Huyton">Huyton</a>, near Liverpool.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The black population in the summer of 1944 was estimated at 150,000, mostly black GIs from America. However, by 1948 the black population was estimated to have been less than 20,000 and did not reach the previous peak of 1944 until 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Learie_Constantine" title="Learie Constantine">Learie Constantine</a>, a West Indian cricketer, was a welfare officer with the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Labour_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Labour (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Labour</a> when he was refused service at a London hotel. He sued for <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_contract" title="Breach of contract">breach of contract</a> and was awarded damages. This particular example is used by some to illustrate the slow change from racism towards acceptance and equality of all citizens in London.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg/220px-HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg/330px-HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg/440px-HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="417" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMT_Empire_Windrush_FL9448.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/HMT_Empire_Windrush" title="HMT Empire Windrush">Empire Windrush</a></i> is extremely important within Black British history; in 1948 it carried the first large wave of Jamaican immigrants to the United Kingdom.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1950 there were probably fewer than 20,000 non-white residents in Britain, almost all born overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> After World War II, the largest influx of Black people occurred, mostly from the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a>. Over a quarter of a million West Indians, the overwhelming majority of them from <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, settled in Britain in less than a decade. In 1951 the population of Caribbean and African-born people in Britain was estimated at 20,900.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-1960s, Britain had become the centre of the largest overseas population of West Indians.<sup id="cite_ref-Writingblack_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Writingblack-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> This migration event is often labelled "Windrush", a reference to the <a href="/wiki/HMT_Empire_Windrush" title="HMT Empire Windrush">HMT&#160;<i>Empire Windrush</i></a>, the ship that carried the first major group of Caribbean migrants to the United Kingdom in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Caribbean" is itself not one ethnic or political identity; for example, some of this wave of immigrants were <a href="/wiki/Indo-Caribbean" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Caribbean">Indo-Caribbean</a>. The most widely used term used at that time was "<a href="/wiki/West_Indian" title="West Indian">West Indian</a>" (or sometimes "coloured"). "Black British" did not come into widespread use until the second generation were born to these post-war migrants to the UK. Although British by nationality, due to friction between them and the white majority they were often born into communities that were relatively closed, creating the roots of what would become a distinct Black British identity. By the 1950s, there was a consciousness of black people as a separate group that had not been there between 1932 and 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-Writingblack_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Writingblack-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The increasing consciousness of Black British peoples was deeply informed by the influx of Black American culture imported by Black servicemen during and after World War II, music being a central example of what Jacqueline Nassy-Brown calls "diasporic resources". These close interactions between Americans and Black British were not only material but also inspired the expatriation of some Black British women to America after marrying servicemen (some of whom later repatriated to the UK).<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
