<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_20th_century">Late 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Late 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/220px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/330px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/440px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="607" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ridley Road Market in <a href="/wiki/Dalston" title="Dalston">Dalston</a>, London, which sells Afro-Caribbean music, textiles, and food including goat meat, yams, mangos and spices.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1961, the population of people born in Africa or the Caribbean was estimated at 191,600, just under 0.4% of the total UK population.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Immigrants_Act_1962" title="Commonwealth Immigrants Act 1962">1962 Commonwealth Immigrants Act</a> was passed in Britain along with a succession of other laws in <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Immigrants_Act_1968" title="Commonwealth Immigrants Act 1968">1968</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_1971" title="Immigration Act 1971">1971</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Nationality_Act_1981" title="British Nationality Act 1981">1981</a>, which severely restricted the entry of Black immigrants into Britain. During this period it is widely argued that emergent blacks and Asians struggled in Britain against racism and prejudice. During the 1970s—and partly in response to both the rise in racial intolerance and the rise of the Black Power movement abroad—"black" became detached from its negative connotations, and was reclaimed as a marker of pride: black is beautiful.<sup id="cite_ref-Writingblack_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Writingblack-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 1975, <a href="/wiki/David_Pitt,_Baron_Pitt_of_Hampstead" title="David Pitt, Baron Pitt of Hampstead">David Pitt</a> was appointed to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. He spoke against racism and for equality in regards to all residents of Britain. In the years that followed, several Black members were elected into the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British Parliament</a>. By 1981, the black population in the United Kingdom was estimated at 1.2% of all countries of birth, being 0.8% Black-Caribbean, 0.3% Black-Other, and 0.1% Black-African residents.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1980s, the majority of black immigrants into the country have come directly from Africa, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> in West Africa, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> in East Africa, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> in Southern Africa. Nigerians and Ghanaians have been especially quick to accustom themselves to British life, with young Nigerians and Ghanaians achieving some of the best results at <a href="/wiki/GCSE" class="mw-redirect" title="GCSE">GCSE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Level_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced Level (UK)">A-Level</a>, often on a par or above the performance of white pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The rate of <a href="/wiki/Inter-racial_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Inter-racial marriage">inter-racial marriage</a> between British citizens born in Africa and native Britons is still fairly low, compared to those from the Caribbean. This might change over time as Africans become more part of mainstream <a href="/wiki/British_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="British culture">British culture</a> as second and third generation African communities become established.
</p><p>By the end of the 20th century the number of black Londoners numbered half a million, according to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_1991" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 1991">1991 census</a>. The 1991 census was the first to include a question on ethnicity, and the black population of Great Britain (i.e. the United Kingdom excluding Northern Ireland, where the question was not asked) was recorded as 890,727, or 1.63% of the total population. This figure included 499,964 people in the Black-Caribbean category (0.9%), 212,362 in the Black-African category (0.4%) and 178,401 in the Black-Other category (0.3%).<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> An increasing number of black Londoners were London- or British-born. Even with this growing population and the first blacks elected to Parliament, many argue that there was still discrimination and a socio-economic imbalance in London among the blacks. In 1992, the number of blacks in Parliament increased to six, and in 1997, they increased their numbers to nine. There are still many problems that black Londoners face; the new global and high-tech information revolution is changing the urban economy and some argue that it is driving up unemployment rates among blacks relative to non-blacks,<sup id="cite_ref-FilePower_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FilePower-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> something, it is argued, that threatens to erode the progress made thus far.<sup id="cite_ref-FilePower_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FilePower-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> By 2001, the Black British population was estimated at 1,148,738 (2.0%).<sup id="cite_ref-Population_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Street_conflicts_and_policing">Street conflicts and policing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Street conflicts and policing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_race_riots" class="mw-redirect" title="List of race riots">List of race riots</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg/220px-1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg/330px-1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg/440px-1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1981_Brixton_Riots.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/1981_Brixton_riot" title="1981 Brixton riot">Brixton race riot</a> in London, 1981.</div></div></div>
<p>The late 1950s through to the late 1980s saw a number of mass street conflicts involving young Afro-Caribbean men and (largely white) British police officers in English cities, mostly as a result of tensions between members of local black communities and white racists.
</p><p>The first major incident occurred in <a href="/wiki/1958_Notting_Hill_race_riots" title="1958 Notting Hill race riots">1958 in Notting Hill</a>, when roaming gangs of between 300 and 400 white youths attacked Afro-Caribbeans and their houses across the neighbourhood, leading to a number of Afro-Caribbean men being left unconscious in the streets.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The following year, Antigua-born <a href="/wiki/Kelso_Cochrane" class="mw-redirect" title="Kelso Cochrane">Kelso Cochrane</a> died after being set upon and stabbed by a gang of white youths while walking home to Notting Hill.
</p><p>During the 1970s, police forces across England increasingly began to use the <a href="/wiki/Sus_law" title="Sus law">Sus law</a>, provoking a sense that young black men were being discriminated against by the police<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The next newsworthy outbreak of street fighting occurred in 1976 at the <a href="/wiki/Notting_Hill_Carnival" title="Notting Hill Carnival">Notting Hill Carnival</a> when several hundred police officers and youths became involved in televised fights and scuffles, with stones thrown at police, baton charges and a number of minor injuries and arrests.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1980_St._Pauls_riot" title="1980 St. Pauls riot">1980 St. Pauls riot</a> in Bristol saw fighting between local youths and police officers, resulting in numerous minor injuries, damage to property and arrests. In London 1981 brought further conflict, with a perceived racist police force after the death of 13 black youngsters who were attending a birthday party that ended in the devastating <a href="/wiki/New_Cross_house_fire" title="New Cross house fire">New Cross Fire</a>. The fire was viewed by many as a racist massacre<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and a major political demonstration, known as the <a href="/wiki/New_Cross_house_fire" title="New Cross house fire">Black People's Day of Action</a> was held to protest against the attacks themselves, a perceived rise in racism, and perceived hostility and indifference from the police, politicians and media.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Tensions were further inflamed when, in nearby <a href="/wiki/1981_Brixton_riot" title="1981 Brixton riot">Brixton</a>, police launched operation Swamp 81, a series of mass stop-and-searches of young black men.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Anger erupted when up to 500 people were involved in street fighting between the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a> and local Afro-Caribbean community, leading to a number of cars and shops set on fire, stones thrown at police and hundreds of arrests and minor injuries. A similar pattern occurred further north in <a href="/wiki/1981_Toxteth_riots" title="1981 Toxteth riots">Toxteth</a>, Liverpool, and <a href="/wiki/Chapeltown,_Leeds" title="Chapeltown, Leeds">Chapeltown, Leeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the recommendations of the <a href="/wiki/Scarman_Report" title="Scarman Report">Scarman Report</a> (published in November 1981),<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> relations between black youths and police did not significantly improve and a further wave of nationwide conflicts occurred in <a href="/wiki/1985_Handsworth_riots" title="1985 Handsworth riots">Handsworth</a>, Birmingham, in 1985, when the local <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">South Asian</a> community also became involved.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Following the police shooting of a black grandmother <a href="/wiki/Cherry_Groce" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherry Groce">Cherry Groce</a> in Brixton, and the death of <a href="/wiki/Cynthia_Jarrett" class="mw-redirect" title="Cynthia Jarrett">Cynthia Jarrett</a> during a raid on her home in <a href="/wiki/Tottenham" title="Tottenham">Tottenham</a>, in north London, protests held at the local police stations did not end peacefully and further street battles with the police erupted,<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> the disturbances later spreading to <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Moss_Side" title="Moss Side">Moss Side</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion_159-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The street battles themselves (involving more stone-throwing, the discharge of one firearm, and several fires) led to two fatalities (in the <a href="/wiki/Broadwater_Farm_riot" title="Broadwater Farm riot">Broadwater Farm riot</a>) and Brixton.
</p><p>In 1999, following the Macpherson Inquiry into the 1993 killing of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Lawrence" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen Lawrence">Stephen Lawrence</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Paul_Condon,_Baron_Condon" title="Paul Condon, Baron Condon">Paul Condon</a>, commissioner of the Metropolitan Police, accepted that his organisation was <a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">institutionally racist</a>. Some members of the Black British community were involved in the <a href="/wiki/2001_Harehills_race_riot" class="mw-redirect" title="2001 Harehills race riot">2001 Harehills race riot</a> and <a href="/wiki/2005_Birmingham_race_riots" class="mw-redirect" title="2005 Birmingham race riots">2005 Birmingham race riots</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_21st_century">Early 21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Early 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg/220px-Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg/330px-Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg/440px-Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carpetright_store_after_Tottenham_riots.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Firefighters douse a shop and flats destroyed by arson during the initial rioting in <a href="/wiki/Tottenham" title="Tottenham">Tottenham</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 2011, following the shooting of a mixed-race man, <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Mark_Duggan" title="Death of Mark Duggan">Mark Duggan</a>, by police in Tottenham, a protest was held at the local police station. The protest ended with an outbreak of fighting between local youths and police officers leading to widespread disturbances across <a href="/wiki/2011_England_riots" title="2011 England riots">English cities</a>.
</p><p>Some analysts claimed that black people were disproportionally represented in the <a href="/wiki/2011_England_riots" title="2011 England riots">2011 England riots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Research suggests that race relations in Britain deteriorated in the period following the riots and that prejudice towards ethnic minorities increased.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/English_Defence_League" title="English Defence League">EDL</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_National_Party" title="British National Party">BNP</a> were said to be exploiting the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Racial tensions between blacks and Asians in Birmingham increased after the deaths of three Asian men at the hands of a black youth.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a <i><a href="/wiki/Newsnight" title="Newsnight">Newsnight</a></i> discussion on 12 August 2011, historian <a href="/wiki/David_Starkey" title="David Starkey">David Starkey</a> blamed black gangster and rap culture, saying that it had influenced youths of all races.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>  Figures showed that 46 per cent of people brought before a courtroom for arrests related to the 2011 riots were black.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG/220px-WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG/330px-WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG/440px-WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WMUK_British_Black_Music_editathon_2013_04.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Community organizers in London.</div></div></div>
<p>The 2011 UK Census recorded 1,904,684 residents who identified as "Black/African/Caribbean/Black British", accounting for 3 per cent of the total UK population.<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This was the first UK census where the number of self-reported Black African residents exceeded that of Black Caribbeans.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within England and Wales, 989,628 individuals specified their ethnicity as "Black African", 594,825 as "Black Caribbean", and 280,437 as "Other Black".<sup id="cite_ref-2011CensusEngWales_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011CensusEngWales-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> In Northern Ireland, 2,345 individuals self-reported as "Black African", 372 as "Black Caribbean", and 899 as "Other Black", totaling 3,616 "Black" residents.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> In Scotland, 29,638 persons identified themselves as "African", choosing either the "African, African Scottish or African British" tick box or the "Other African" tick box and write-in area. 6,540 individuals also self-reported as "Caribbean or Black", selecting either the "Caribbean, Caribbean Scottish or Caribbean British" tick box, the "Black, Black Scottish or Black British" tick box, or the "Other Caribbean or Black" tick box and write-in area.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In order to compare UK-wide results, the Office for National Statistics combined the "African" and "Caribbean or Black" entries at the top-level,<sup id="cite_ref-Onseg_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onseg-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and reported a total of 36,178 "Black" residents in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> According to the ONS, individuals in Scotland with "Other African", "White" and "Asian" ethnicities as well as "Black" identities could thus all potentially be captured within this combined output.<sup id="cite_ref-Onseg_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onseg-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The General Register Office for Scotland, which devised the categories and administers the Scotland census, does not combine the "African" and "Caribbean or Black" entries, maintaining them as separate for individuals who do not self-identify as "Black" (see <a href="/wiki/Black_British#Census_classification" title="Black British">census classification</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Snoec_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snoec-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/2001_UK_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="2001 UK Census">2001 Census</a>, 575,876 people in the United Kingdom had reported their ethnicity as "Black Caribbean", 485,277 as "Black African", and 97,585 as "Black Other", making a total of 1,148,738 "Black or Black British" residents. This was equivalent to 2 per cent of the UK population at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Population_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_distribution">Population distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Population distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg/220px-Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg/330px-Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg/440px-Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghanaian_Independence_Celebrations_-_geograph.org.uk_-_376356.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghanaian</a> independence day celebrations in the <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Barnet" title="London Borough of Barnet">London Borough of Barnet</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most Black Britons can be found in the large cities and metropolitan areas of the country. The 2011 census found that 1.85 million of a total Black population of 1.9 million lived in England, with 1.09 million of those in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, where they made up 13.3 per cent of the population, compared to 3.5 per cent of England's population and 3 per cent of the UK's population. The ten local authorities with the highest proportion of their populations describing themselves as Black in the census were all in London: <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Lewisham" title="London Borough of Lewisham">Lewisham</a> (27.2 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Southwark" title="London Borough of Southwark">Southwark</a> (26.9 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Lambeth" title="London Borough of Lambeth">Lambeth</a> (25.9 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Hackney" title="London Borough of Hackney">Hackney</a> (23.1 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Croydon" title="London Borough of Croydon">Croydon</a> (20.2 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Barking_and_Dagenham" title="London Borough of Barking and Dagenham">Barking and Dagenham</a> (20.0 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Newham" title="London Borough of Newham">Newham</a> (19.6 per cent), <a href="/wiki/Royal_Borough_of_Greenwich" title="Royal Borough of Greenwich">Greenwich</a> (19.1 per cent), <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Haringey" title="London Borough of Haringey">Haringey</a> (18.8 per cent) and <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Brent" title="London Borough of Brent">Brent</a> (18.8 per cent).<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of London, the next largest populations are in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> (96,360, 9%) / <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton" title="Wolverhampton">Wolverhampton</a> (17,309, 6.9%), <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> (43,484, 8.6%), Leeds (25,893, 3.45%), <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> (25,734, 6%), <a href="/wiki/Nottingham" title="Nottingham">Nottingham</a> (22,185, 7.3%), <a href="/wiki/Leicester" title="Leicester">Leicester</a> (20,585, 6.2%) and <a href="/wiki/Luton" title="Luton">Luton</a> (19,909, 9.8%).<sup id="cite_ref-2011census_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011census-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_marriages">Mixed marriages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mixed marriages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An academic journal article published in 2005, citing sources from 1997 and 2001, estimated that nearly half of British-born <a href="/wiki/British_African-Caribbean_community" class="mw-redirect" title="British African-Caribbean community">African-Caribbean</a> men, a third of British-born African-Caribbean women, and a fifth of <a href="/wiki/African_migration_to_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="African migration to the United Kingdom">African</a> men, have white partners.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> reported that, according to the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Force_Survey" title="Labour Force Survey">Labour Force Survey</a>, 48 per cent of black Caribbean men and 34 per cent of black Caribbean women in couples have partners from a different ethnic group. Moreover, mixed-race children under the age of ten with black Caribbean and white parents outnumber black Caribbean children by two-to-one.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_community">Culture and community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Culture and community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg/220px-Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg/330px-Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg/440px-Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Notting_Hill_Carnival_2004_004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Notting_Hill_Carnival" title="Notting Hill Carnival">Notting Hill Carnival</a> in London.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect">Dialect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dialect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multicultural_London_English" title="Multicultural London English">Multicultural London English</a></div>
<p>Multicultural London English is a variety of the English language spoken by a large number of the Black British population of Afro-Caribbean ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> British Black dialect has been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_Patois" title="Jamaican Patois">Jamaican Patois</a> owing to the large number of immigrants from Jamaica, but it is also spoken or imitated by those of different ancestry.
</p><p>British Black speech is also heavily influenced by social class and regional dialect (<a href="/wiki/Cockney" title="Cockney">Cockney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_dialect" title="Manchester dialect">Mancunian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brummie" class="mw-redirect" title="Brummie">Brummie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scouse" title="Scouse">Scouse</a>, etc.).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_music_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Caribbean music in the United Kingdom">Caribbean music in the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Black_music" title="British Black music">British Black music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_musicians" title="Category:Black British musicians">Category:Black British musicians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_singers" title="Category:Black British singers">Category:Black British singers</a></div>
<p>Black British music is a long-established and influential part of <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Music of the United Kingdom">British music</a>. Its presence in the United Kingdom stretches back to the 18th century, encompassing concert performers such as <a href="/wiki/George_Bridgetower" title="George Bridgetower">George Bridgetower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Busking" class="mw-redirect" title="Busking">street musicians</a> the likes of <a href="/wiki/Billy_Waters_(busker)" title="Billy Waters (busker)">Billy Waters</a>. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Coleridge-Taylor" title="Samuel Coleridge-Taylor">Samuel Coleridge-Taylor</a> (1875–1912) achieved great success as a composer at the end of the 19th and early 20th centuries.
</p><p>In the late 1970s and 1980s, <a href="/wiki/2_Tone_(music_genre)" class="mw-redirect" title="2 Tone (music genre)">2 Tone</a> became popular with the <a href="/wiki/Youth_culture" title="Youth culture">British youth</a>; especially in the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_conurbation" title="West Midlands conurbation">West Midlands</a>. A blend of <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ska" title="Ska">ska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> made it a favourite among both white and black audiences. Famous bands in the genre include <a href="/wiki/The_Selecter" title="The Selecter">The Selecter</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Specials" title="The Specials">The Specials</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Beat_(British_band)" title="The Beat (British band)">The Beat</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Bodysnatchers_(band)" title="The Bodysnatchers (band)">The Bodysnatchers</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Waters,_a_one_legged_busker,_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg/220px-Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg/330px-Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg/440px-Billy_Waters%2C_a_one_legged_busker%2C_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3223" data-file-height="2173" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Billy_Waters,_a_one_legged_busker,_in_a_crowded_London_stree_Wellcome_V0007299.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Billy_Waters_(busker)" title="Billy Waters (busker)">Billy Waters</a> busking in London during the early 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oldschool_jungle" class="mw-redirect" title="Oldschool jungle">Jungle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dubstep" title="Dubstep">dubstep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drum_and_bass" title="Drum and bass">drum and bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/UK_garage" title="UK garage">UK garage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grime_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grime (music)">grime</a> music originated in London and involve a number of artists from primarily Caribbean communities but recently Black Africans also, most notably of Ghanaian and Nigerian origin. Famous grime artists include <a href="/wiki/Dizzee_Rascal" title="Dizzee Rascal">Dizzee Rascal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tinchy_Stryder" title="Tinchy Stryder">Tinchy Stryder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tinie_Tempah" title="Tinie Tempah">Tinie Tempah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chipmunk_(rapper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chipmunk (rapper)">Chipmunk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kano_(rapper)" title="Kano (rapper)">Kano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wiley_(rapper)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wiley (rapper)">Wiley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lethal_Bizzle" title="Lethal Bizzle">Lethal Bizzle</a>. It is now common to hear British <a href="/wiki/Rapping" title="Rapping">MCs</a> rapping in a strong London accent. <a href="/wiki/Bassline_(dance_music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bassline (dance music)">Niche</a>, with its origin in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, has a much faster bassline and is often sung in a northern accent. Famous niche artists include producer <a href="/wiki/T2_(producer)" title="T2 (producer)">T2</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/MOBO_Awards" title="MOBO Awards">MOBO Awards</a> – recognizing performers of "Music of Black Origin" – are seen as a UK equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/BET_Awards" title="BET Awards">BET Awards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soul_Train_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul Train Awards">Soul Train Awards</a> for being the main award show in Britain to focus on urban music.
</p><p>Among Black British artists to have gained significant popularity in the U.S. are <a href="/wiki/Sade_(singer)" title="Sade (singer)">Sade</a> ("Paradise"), <a href="/wiki/Soul_II_Soul" title="Soul II Soul">Soul II Soul</a> ("Back to Life"), <a href="/wiki/Des%27ree" title="Des&#39;ree">Des'ree</a> ("<a href="/wiki/You_Gotta_Be" title="You Gotta Be">You Gotta Be</a>"), and <a href="/wiki/Loose_Ends_(band)" title="Loose Ends (band)">Loose Ends</a> ("Hangin' On A String").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The black community in Britain has a number of significant publications. The leading key publication is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Voice_(British_newspaper)" title="The Voice (British newspaper)">The Voice</a></i> newspaper, founded by <a href="/wiki/Val_McCalla" title="Val McCalla">Val McCalla</a> in 1982, and Britain's only national Black weekly newspaper. <i>The Voice</i> primarily targets the Caribbean diaspora and has been printed for more than 35 years.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Secondly, the<i>Black History Month</i> magazine is a central point of focus which leads the nationwide celebration of Black History, Arts and Culture throughout the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_Magazine" title="Pride Magazine">Pride Magazine</a></i>, published since 1991, is the largest monthly magazine that targets black British, mixed-race, African and African-Caribbean women in the United Kingdom. In 2007, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that the magazine had dominated the black women's magazine market for over 15&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> <i>Keep The Faith</i> magazine is a multi-award winning Black and minority ethnic community magazine produced quarterly since 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> <i>Keep The Faith</i>’s editorial contributors are some of the most powerful and influential movers and shakers, and successful entrepreneurs within BME communities.
</p><p>Many major Black British publications are handled through Diverse Media Group,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> which specialises in helping organisations reach Britain's Black and minority ethnic community through the main media they consume. The senior leadership team is an composite of many CEO and owners from the publications listed above
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_issues">Social issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Social issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diane_Abbott,_New_Statesman_hustings,_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg/220px-Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg/330px-Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg/440px-Diane_Abbott%2C_New_Statesman_hustings%2C_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diane_Abbott,_New_Statesman_hustings,_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Diane_Abbott" title="Diane Abbott">Diane Abbott</a> ran for <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">leadership</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> in <a href="/wiki/2010_Labour_Party_leadership_election_(UK)" title="2010 Labour Party leadership election (UK)">2010</a>.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Racism">Racism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Racism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The wave of black immigrants who arrived in Britain from the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> in the 1950s faced significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>. For many Caribbean immigrants, their first experience of discrimination came when trying to find private accommodation. They were generally ineligible for <a href="/wiki/Council_housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Council housing">council housing</a> because only people who had been resident in the UK for a minimum of five years qualified for it. At the time, there was no anti-discrimination legislation to prevent landlords from refusing to accept black tenants. A survey undertaken in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> in 1956 found that only 15 of a total of 1,000 white people surveyed would let a room to a black tenant. As a result, many black immigrants were forced to live in <a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slum</a> areas of cities, where the housing was of poor quality and there were problems of crime, violence and prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Cloake_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cloake-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> One of the most notorious slum landlords was <a href="/wiki/Peter_Rachman" title="Peter Rachman">Peter Rachman</a>, who owned around 100 properties in the <a href="/wiki/Notting_Hill" title="Notting Hill">Notting Hill</a> area of London. Black tenants typically paid twice the rent of white tenants, and lived in conditions of extreme overcrowding.<sup id="cite_ref-Cloake_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cloake-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Winston James argues that the experience of racism in Britain was a major factor in the development of a shared Caribbean identity amongst black immigrants from a range of different island and class backgrounds.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s and 1980s, black people in Britain were the victims of racist violence perpetrated by <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> groups such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> During this period, it was also common for Black <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">footballers</a> to be subjected to racist chanting from crowd members.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Racism in Britain in general, including against black people, is considered to have declined over time. Academic Robert Ford demonstrates that <a href="/wiki/Social_distance" title="Social distance">social distance</a>, measured using questions from the <a href="/wiki/British_Social_Attitudes" class="mw-redirect" title="British Social Attitudes">British Social Attitudes</a> survey about whether people would mind having an ethnic minority boss or have a close relative marry an ethnic minority spouse, declined over the period 1983–1996. These declines were observed for attitudes towards Black and Asian ethnic minorities. Much of this change in attitudes happened in the 1990s. In the 1980s, opposition to interracial marriage were significant.<sup id="cite_ref-Ford2008_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford2008-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, Ford argues that "Racism and racial discrimination remain a part of everyday life for Britain's ethnic minorities. Black and Asian Britons...are less likely to be employed and are more likely to work in worse jobs, live in worse houses and suffer worse health than White Britons".<sup id="cite_ref-Ford2008_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford2008-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Maryland">University of Maryland</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Minorities_at_Risk" title="Minorities at Risk">Minorities at Risk</a> (MAR) project noted in 2006 that while African-Caribbeans in the United Kingdom no longer face formal discrimination, they continue to be under-represented in politics, and to face discriminatory barriers in access to housing and in employment practices. The project also notes that the British school system "has been indicted on numerous occasions for racism, and for undermining the self-confidence of black children and maligning the culture of their parents". The MAR profile on African-Caribbeans in the United Kingdom notes "growing 'black on black' violence between people from the Caribbean and immigrants from Africa".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is concern that murders using knives are given insufficient attention because most victims are black.  Martin Hewitt of the Metropolitan Police said, "I do fear sometimes that because the majority of those that are injured or killed are coming from certain communities and very often the black communities in London, it doesn’t get the sense of collective outrage that it ought to do and really get everyone to a place where we are all doing everything we can to prevent this from happening.  It’s an enormous effort on our part. We are putting enormous resources in to try and stem the flow of the violence and having some success at doing that. But collectively we all ought to be looking at this and seeing how we can prevent it."<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unemployment">Unemployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Unemployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the 2005 <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">TUC</a> report <i>Black workers, jobs and poverty</i>, Black and minority ethnic people (BMEs) were far more likely to be unemployed than the white population. The rate of unemployment among the white population was only 12%, but among black groups it was 16%, mixed-race 15%, Indian 7%, Pakistani 15%, Bangladeshi 17% and Chinese 5%. The rates of poverty and low income were twice to three times higher, of the different ethnic groups studied, Bangladeshis, Pakistanis and Black British had the highest rates of child poverty of over 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 study by the Black Training and Enterprise Group (BTEG), funded by <a href="/wiki/Trust_for_London" title="Trust for London">Trust for London</a>, explored the views of young Black males in London on why their demographic have a higher unemployment rate than any other group of young people, finding that many young black men in London believe that racism and negative stereotyping are the main reasons for their high unemployment rate.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">Crime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Crime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Both racist crime and gang-related crime continues to affect black communities, so much so that the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">Metropolitan Police</a> launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Trident_(Metropolitan_Police)" title="Operation Trident (Metropolitan Police)">Operation Trident</a> to tackle black-on-black crimes. Numerous deaths in police custody of black men has generated a general distrust of police among urban blacks in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> According to the Metropolitan Police Authority in 2002–03 of the 17 deaths in police custody, 10 were black or Asian – black convicts have a disproportionately higher rate of incarceration than other ethnicities. The government reports<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The overall number of racist incidents recorded by the police rose by 7 per cent from 49,078 in 2002/03 to 52,694 in 2003/04.
</p><p>Media representation of young black British people has focused particularly on "gangs" with black members and violent crimes involving black victims and perpetrators.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> According to a Home Office report,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> 10 per cent of all murder victims between 2000 and 2004 were black. Of these, 56 per cent were murdered by other black people (with 44 per cent of black people murdered by whites and Asians – making black people disproportionately higher victims of killing by people from other ethnicities). In addition, a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_request" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom of Information request">Freedom of Information request</a> made by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> shows internal police data that provides a breakdown of the ethnicity of the 18,091 men and boys who police took action against for a range of offences in London in October 2009. Among those proceeded against for street crimes, 54 per cent were black; for robbery, 59 per cent; and for gun crimes, 67 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Black people, who according to government statistics<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> make up 2 per cent of the population, are the principal suspects in 11.7 per cent of murders, i.e.&#160;in 252 out of 2163 murders committed 2001/2, 2002/3, and 2003/4.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Judging on the basis of prison population, a substantial minority (about 35%) of black criminals in the United Kingdom are not British citizens but <a href="/wiki/Foreign_national" title="Foreign national">foreign nationals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In November 2009, the Home Office published a study that showed that, once other variables had been <a href="/wiki/Controlling_for_a_variable" title="Controlling for a variable">accounted for</a>, ethnicity was not a significant predictor of offending, anti-social behaviour or drug abuse among young people.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After several high-profile investigations such as that of the <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Stephen_Lawrence" title="Murder of Stephen Lawrence">murder of Stephen Lawrence</a>, the police have been accused of racism, from both within and outside the service. <a href="/wiki/Cressida_Dick" title="Cressida Dick">Cressida Dick</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>'s anti-racism unit in 2003, remarked that it was "difficult to imagine a situation where we will say we are no longer <a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">institutionally racist</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Black people were seven times more likely to be stopped and searched by police compared to white people, according to the Home Office, A separate study said blacks were more than nine times more likely to be searched.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though blacks are only 2 to 3% of the general UK population, black prisoners make up 15% of the British prison population, which experts say is "a result of decades of racial prejudice in the criminal justice system and an overly punitive approach to penal affairs."<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Black boys and men also make up 51% of inmates in young offender institutions (YOIs), despite being just 14% of the overall BME population.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_black_Britons">Notable black Britons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Notable black Britons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-20th_century">Pre-20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Pre-20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seacole_-_Challen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Seacole_-_Challen.jpg/220px-Seacole_-_Challen.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Seacole_-_Challen.jpg/330px-Seacole_-_Challen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Seacole_-_Challen.jpg/440px-Seacole_-_Challen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seacole_-_Challen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crimean War nurse, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Seacole" title="Mary Seacole">Mary Seacole</a> has been dubbed the "Greatest Black Briton."</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png/220px-Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="354" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png/330px-Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png/440px-Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Olaudah_Equiano_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_15399.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Olaudah_Equiano" title="Olaudah Equiano">Olaudah Equiano</a>, a significant figure involved with the abolition of the Atlantic Slave Trade.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Sentamu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_Sentamu.jpg/220px-John_Sentamu.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_Sentamu.jpg/330px-John_Sentamu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/John_Sentamu.jpg/440px-John_Sentamu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="820" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Sentamu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Sentamu" title="John Sentamu">John Sentamu</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, the second most senior clerical position in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> after that of <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Justin Welby</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Well-known black Britons living before the 20th century include the <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartist</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Cuffay" title="William Cuffay">William Cuffay</a>; <a href="/wiki/William_Davidson_(conspirator)" title="William Davidson (conspirator)">William Davidson</a>, who was executed as a <a href="/wiki/Cato_Street_Conspiracy" title="Cato Street Conspiracy">Cato Street</a> conspirator; <a href="/wiki/Olaudah_Equiano" title="Olaudah Equiano">Olaudah Equiano</a> (also called Gustavus Vassa), a former slave who <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">bought his freedom</a>, moved to England, and settled in <a href="/wiki/Soham" title="Soham">Soham</a>, Cambridgeshire, where he married and wrote an autobiography, dying in 1797; <a href="/wiki/Ukawsaw_Gronniosaw" title="Ukawsaw Gronniosaw">Ukawsaw Gronniosaw</a>, pioneer of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_narrative" title="Slave narrative">slave narrative</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a>, a grocer who also acquired a reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Man_of_letters" class="mw-redirect" title="Man of letters">man of letters</a>.
</p><p>In 2004, a poll found that people considered the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> heroine <a href="/wiki/Mary_Seacole" title="Mary Seacole">Mary Seacole</a> to be the greatest Black Briton.<sup id="cite_ref-Seacole_statue_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seacole_statue-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Seacole was born in Jamaica in 1805 to a white father and black mother.<sup id="cite_ref-Seacole_profile_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seacole_profile-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> A statue of her, designed by Martin Jennings, was unveiled in the grounds of <a href="/wiki/St._Thomas%27_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Thomas&#39; Hospital">St. Thomas' Hospital</a> opposite the <a href="/wiki/Houses_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Houses of Parliament">Houses of Parliament</a> in London in June 2016, following a 12-year campaign that raised £500,000 to honour her.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_television_personalities" title="Category:Black British television personalities">Category:Black British television personalities</a></div>
<p>Television reporter and newsreader Sir <a href="/wiki/Trevor_McDonald" title="Trevor McDonald">Trevor McDonald</a>, born in <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a>, was knighted in 1999. Also notable is <a href="/wiki/Moira_Stuart" title="Moira Stuart">Moira Stuart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_British_Empire" title="Order of the British Empire">OBE</a>, the first female newsreader of African-Caribbean heritage on British television. Other high-profile television personalities and entertainers include <a href="/wiki/Sir_Lenny_Henry" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Lenny Henry">Sir Lenny Henry</a> and chef <a href="/wiki/Ainsley_Harriott" title="Ainsley Harriott">Ainsley Harriott</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singers">Singers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Singers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joan_Armatrading" title="Joan Armatrading">Joan Armatrading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Bassey" title="Shirley Bassey">Shirley Bassey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taio_Cruz" title="Taio Cruz">Taio Cruz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Des%27ree" title="Des&#39;ree">Des'ree</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabrielle_(singer)" title="Gabrielle (singer)">Gabrielle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roland_Gift" title="Roland Gift">Roland Gift</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaki_Graham" title="Jaki Graham">Jaki Graham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Grant" title="Eddy Grant">Eddy Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Henry" title="Pauline Henry">Pauline Henry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamelia" title="Jamelia">Jamelia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leona_Lewis" title="Leona Lewis">Leona Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maxine_Nightingale" title="Maxine Nightingale">Maxine Nightingale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Ocean" title="Billy Ocean">Billy Ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maxi_Priest" title="Maxi Priest">Maxi Priest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roachford" title="Andrew Roachford">Andrew Roachford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sade_(singer)" title="Sade (singer)">Sade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emeli_Sande" class="mw-redirect" title="Emeli Sande">Emeli Sande</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seal_(singer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal (singer)">Seal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heather_Small" title="Heather Small">Heather Small</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benji_Webbe" title="Benji Webbe">Benji Webbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/21_Savage" title="21 Savage">21 Savage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Webbe" title="Simon Webbe">Simon Webbe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caron_Wheeler" title="Caron Wheeler">Caron Wheeler</a> are among the popular singers not mentioned in the music section above.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Initially receiving acclaim as a visual artist and winning the <a href="/wiki/Turner_Prize" title="Turner Prize">Turner Prize</a> in 1999, <a href="/wiki/Steve_McQueen_(director)" title="Steve McQueen (director)">Steve McQueen</a> went on to direct his first feature <i><a href="/wiki/Hunger_(2008_film)" title="Hunger (2008 film)">Hunger</a></i> (2008), which earned him the <a href="/wiki/Cam%C3%A9ra_d%27Or" title="Caméra d&#39;Or">Caméra d'Or</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2008_Cannes_Film_Festival" title="2008 Cannes Film Festival">2008 Cannes Film Festival</a>. His most recent film, <i><a href="/wiki/12_Years_a_Slave_(film)" title="12 Years a Slave (film)">12 Years a Slave</a></i> (2013), won several major international awards, and McQueen is the first black filmmaker to win an <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Picture" title="Academy Award for Best Picture">Academy Award for Best Picture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Actors">Actors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Actors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_actors" title="Category:Black British actors">Category:Black British actors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_actresses" title="Category:Black British actresses">Category:Black British actresses</a></div>
<p>Numerous Black British actors have become successful in US television, such as <a href="/wiki/Adewale_Akinnuoye-Agbaje" title="Adewale Akinnuoye-Agbaje">Adewale Akinnuoye-Agbaje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idris_Elba" title="Idris Elba">Idris Elba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lennie_James" title="Lennie James">Lennie James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsha_Thomason" title="Marsha Thomason">Marsha Thomason</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Jean-Baptiste" title="Marianne Jean-Baptiste">Marianne Jean-Baptiste</a>. Black British actors are also increasingly found starring in major Hollywood movies, notable examples include <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Lester" title="Adrian Lester">Adrian Lester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonia_Thomas" title="Antonia Thomas">Antonia Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashley_Walters_(actor)" title="Ashley Walters (actor)">Ashley Walters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiwetel_Ejiofor" title="Chiwetel Ejiofor">Chiwetel Ejiofor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Salmon" title="Colin Salmon">Colin Salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kaluuya" title="Daniel Kaluuya">Daniel Kaluuya</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Harewood" title="David Harewood">David Harewood</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Oyelowo" title="David Oyelowo">David Oyelowo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delroy_Lindo" title="Delroy Lindo">Delroy Lindo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eamonn_Walker" title="Eamonn Walker">Eamonn Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Drameh" title="Franz Drameh">Franz Drameh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gugu_Mbatha-Raw" title="Gugu Mbatha-Raw">Gugu Mbatha-Raw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Quarshie" title="Hugh Quarshie">Hugh Quarshie</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Boyega" title="John Boyega">John Boyega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maisie_Richardson-Sellers" title="Maisie Richardson-Sellers">Maisie Richardson-Sellers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomie_Harris" title="Naomie Harris">Naomie Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Okonedo" title="Sophie Okonedo">Sophie Okonedo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eunice_Olumide" title="Eunice Olumide">Eunice Olumide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thandie_Newton" title="Thandie Newton">Thandie Newton</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_artists">Visual artists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Visual artists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_artists" title="Category:Black British artists">Category:Black British artists</a></div>
<p>Among notable Black British visual artists are painters such as <a href="/wiki/Chris_Ofili" title="Chris Ofili">Chris Ofili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bowling" title="Frank Bowling">Frank Bowling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keith_Piper_(artist)" title="Keith Piper (artist)">Keith Piper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Boyce" title="Sonia Boyce">Sonia Boyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dash" title="Paul Dash">Paul Dash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kimathi_Donkor" title="Kimathi Donkor">Kimathi Donkor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudette_Johnson" title="Claudette Johnson">Claudette Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Branch" title="Winston Branch">Winston Branch</a>, and sculptors including <a href="/wiki/Sokari_Douglas_Camp" title="Sokari Douglas Camp">Sokari Douglas Camp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Moody" title="Ronald Moody">Ronald Moody</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fowokan" title="Fowokan">Fowokan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yinka_Shonibare" title="Yinka Shonibare">Yinka Shonibare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zak_Ov%C3%A9" title="Zak Ové">Zak Ové</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writers">Writers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Writers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_writers" title="Category:Black British writers">Category:Black British writers</a></div>
<p>Notable Black British writers include novelists <a href="/wiki/Caryl_Phillips" title="Caryl Phillips">Caryl Phillips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zadie_Smith" title="Zadie Smith">Zadie Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Levy" title="Andrea Levy">Andrea Levy</a> (whose 2004 book <i><a href="/wiki/Small_Island_(novel)" title="Small Island (novel)">Small Island</a></i> won the <a href="/wiki/Whitbread_Book_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitbread Book of the Year">Whitbread Book of the Year</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Prize_for_Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange Prize for Fiction">Orange Prize for Fiction</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Writers%27_Prize" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth Writers&#39; Prize">Commonwealth Writers' Prize</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bernardine_Evaristo" title="Bernardine Evaristo">Bernardine Evaristo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alex_Wheatle" title="Alex Wheatle">Alex Wheatle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Dennis" title="Ferdinand Dennis">Ferdinand Dennis</a> (winner of the <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Memorial_Prize" title="Martin Luther King Memorial Prize">Martin Luther King Memorial Prize</a> for his 1988 travelogue <i>Behind the Frontlines: Journey into Afro-Britain</i>), <a href="/wiki/Mike_Phillips_(writer)" title="Mike Phillips (writer)">Mike Phillips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diran_Adebayo" title="Diran Adebayo">Diran Adebayo</a> (first winner in 1995 of the Saga Prize, which was set up by <a href="/wiki/Marsha_Hunt_(actress,_born_1946)" title="Marsha Hunt (actress, born 1946)">Marsha Hunt</a> to encourage Black British writing and ran for four years),<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> poets <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Zephaniah" title="Benjamin Zephaniah">Benjamin Zephaniah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linton_Kwesi_Johnson" title="Linton Kwesi Johnson">Linton Kwesi Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemn_Sissay" title="Lemn Sissay">Lemn Sissay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salena_Godden" title="Salena Godden">Salena Godden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patience_Agbabi" title="Patience Agbabi">Patience Agbabi</a>, playwrights <a href="/wiki/Mustapha_Matura" title="Mustapha Matura">Mustapha Matura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Kwei-Armah" title="Kwame Kwei-Armah">Kwame Kwei-Armah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Williams_(playwright)" title="Roy Williams (playwright)">Roy Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winsome_Pinnock" title="Winsome Pinnock">Winsome Pinnock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Cumper" title="Patricia Cumper">Patricia Cumper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bola_Agbaje" title="Bola Agbaje">Bola Agbaje</a>, journalists such as <a href="/wiki/Gary_Younge" title="Gary Younge">Gary Younge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ekow_Eshun" title="Ekow Eshun">Ekow Eshun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Laureate" title="Children&#39;s Laureate">Children's Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Malorie_Blackman" title="Malorie Blackman">Malorie Blackman</a>. <a href="/wiki/Onyeka" title="Onyeka">Onyeka Nubia</a> is the author of fictional trilogy <i>Waiting to Explode</i>, <i>The Black Prince</i>, and <i>The Phoenix</i>, for which he won the 2009 African Achievers award for Communication and Media. <i><a href="/wiki/Blackamoores:_Africans_in_Tudor_England,_their_Presence,_Status_and_Origins" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackamoores: Africans in Tudor England, their Presence, Status and Origins">Blackamoores: Africans in Tudor England, their Presence, Status and Origins</a></i> is his latest book, published by Narrative Eye<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> in 2013, in which he proves that Black people in Tudor England had free status and were not slaves. <i>Blackamoores</i> was runner-up in the 2013/14 People's Book Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_service">Police service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Police service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Fuller" title="Michael Fuller">Michael Fuller</a>, after a career in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>, served as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Constable" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Constable">Chief Constable</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> 2004-2010. He is the son of Jamaican immigrants who came to the United Kingdom in the 1950s. Fuller was brought up in <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a>, where his interest in the police force was encouraged by an officer attached to his school. He is a graduate in <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_services">Military services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Military services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, soldier <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Beharry" title="Johnson Beharry">Johnson Beharry</a>, born in <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a>, became the first man to win the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a>, the United Kingdom's foremost military award for bravery, since the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> of 1982. He was awarded the medal for service in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> in 2004.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Black_British&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Black_British_sportspeople" title="Category:Black British sportspeople">Category:Black British sportspeople</a></div>
<p>In sport, prominent examples of success include boxing champion <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bruno" title="Frank Bruno">Frank Bruno</a>, whose career highlight was winning the WBC <a href="/wiki/List_of_WBC_world_champions" title="List of WBC world champions">world heavyweight championship</a> in 1995. Altogether, he has won 40 of his 45 contests. He is also well known for acting in <a href="/wiki/Pantomime" title="Pantomime">pantomime</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lennox_Lewis" title="Lennox Lewis">Lennox Lewis</a>, born in east London, is another successful Black British boxer and former undisputed heavyweight champion of the world.
</p><p>There are many notable black British footballers, some of whom have played for <a href="/wiki/England_national_football_team" title="England national football team">England</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Rashford" title="Marcus Rashford">Marcus Rashford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ince" title="Paul Ince">Paul Ince</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sol_Campbell" title="Sol Campbell">Sol Campbell</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Barnes_(footballer)" title="John Barnes (footballer)">John Barnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dion_Dublin" title="Dion Dublin">Dion Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rio_Ferdinand" title="Rio Ferdinand">Rio Ferdinand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viv_Anderson" title="Viv Anderson">Viv Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Des_Walker" title="Des Walker">Des Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashley_Cole" title="Ashley Cole">Ashley Cole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_Wright" title="Ian Wright">Ian Wright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Sturridge" title="Daniel Sturridge">Daniel Sturridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Welbeck" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Welbeck">Daniel Welbeck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raheem_Sterling" title="Raheem Sterling">Raheem Sterling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Lingard" title="Jesse Lingard">Jesse Lingard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Rose_(footballer,_born_1990)" title="Danny Rose (footballer, born 1990)">Danny Rose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Bertrand" title="Ryan Bertrand">Ryan Bertrand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Walker" title="Kyle Walker">Kyle Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dele_Alli" title="Dele Alli">Dele Alli</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_James_(footballer)" class="mw-redirect" title="David James (footballer)">David James</a>. <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Watson_(footballer,_born_1856)" title="Andrew Watson (footballer, born 1856)">Andrew Watson</a> who is widely considered to be the world's first association footballer of black heritage, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Iwelumo" title="Chris Iwelumo">Chris Iwelumo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jai_Quitongo" title="Jai Quitongo">Jai Quitongo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zak_Jules" title="Zak Jules">Zak Jules</a> have all played for <a href="/wiki/Scotland_national_football_team" title="Scotland national football team">Scotland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Parris" title="Eddie Parris">Eddie Parris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Gabbidon" title="Danny Gabbidon">Danny Gabbidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Blake" title="Nathan Blake">Nathan Blake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashley_Williams_(footballer)" title="Ashley Williams (footballer)">Ashley Williams</a> have played for <a href="/wiki/Wales_national_football_team" title="Wales national football team">Wales</a>.
